<div id="review_13237607" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/13237607" />
    <a title="Ika" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/481947-ika"><img alt="Ika" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1340769099p2/481947.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/13237607?book_show_action=true">Jan 22, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ika" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ika" href="/user/show/481947-ika">Ika</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer13237607" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16296509332668275295">Nikki (or Coco) is a writer in China which was not popular enough. She is a tough woman who lives on her own. She had a boyfriend named Tian Tian and she lived together with him. Tian Tian was a nice weak loyal guy who loved painting. They both lived happily although Tian Tian could not give her offspring. He was impotent and Coco explained it really clear that they do not mind with that.<br/><br/>Coco has many great friends beside her. They sometimes asked Coco hang out and spend the night together. Coco</span>
  <span id="freeText16296509332668275295" style="display:none">Nikki (or Coco) is a writer in China which was not popular enough. She is a tough woman who lives on her own. She had a boyfriend named Tian Tian and she lived together with him. Tian Tian was a nice weak loyal guy who loved painting. They both lived happily although Tian Tian could not give her offspring. He was impotent and Coco explained it really clear that they do not mind with that.<br /><br />Coco has many great friends beside her. They sometimes asked Coco hang out and spend the night together. Coco has an ordinary day life but she has an exciting nightlife. One day, Coco met Mark. He is a Germany guy with a beautiful wife (Eva) and fabulous son. He has normal body which could satisfy Coco on bed. They sometimes meet and make love without any feeling. Neither does he, Coco does not love Mark. Tian Tian and Eva did not know about their habitual activity at all. So they kept doing it.<br /><br />Tian Tian should go abroad to learn more about painting. He should stay there, at the south, about two month. Coco encouraged him to go and promised him that she would be alright. But the facts, she missed him a lot. Tian Tian also could not live far away from Coco. He was addicted by drugs then.<br /><br />Coco felt very miserable. She regretted the day when she met Mark. She deceived Tian Tian and felt repellent about herself. She went south visiting Tian Tian but she seemed could not help him anymore. In the end of the story, Tian Tian died and Mark went back to Germany with his family. And Coco, she finally finished her novel. She still has many books to write also.<br /><br /><br /><br />I think this story tells about Wei Hui’s passion. She wrote her dreams, her mind and her opinion about life. She is the first Chinese writer who describes state of affairs wildly and clearly (it is written on the testimony about this book). She exposes her sexual life and her social interactions without any boundaries. She describes it through Coco. Wei Hui could write explicitly about life and sex. That might be the reason why her 40.000 books was burnt in front of the public and was told as a communist. But as the flowers bloom, her books glows and touch the heart of many people outside. Here is the quote I took from “Arts Today”:<br /><br />"Shanghai Baby" was published by China's biggest publisher in 1999 and soon sold over 130,000 copies. The official Xinhau (Shin-wha) news agency praised the book as a hugely popular account of the lives of China's "new generation". <br /><br /><br />But then everything changed. The Chinese Government condemned the book as a piece of western decadence - which it certainly is. The book was banned. Two key officials of the publishing company were sacked, and thousands of copies were burnt. As a result of all this notoriety, pirate editions can be easily obtained in China for around A$1.00. <br /><br /><br />Wei Hui sees herself as the voice of a new generation - of a new kind of woman - tired of the old restrictions, and lured by the individualism and the merchandise of the west. She is quite open about her desire to become famous - a desire she is well on her way to realising.”<br /><br />Actually when I read this book from the beginning, I could not find the highlight. Everything flows unruffled and no climax. Shanghai Baby is neither a novel nor a story, it’s an everyday life. No one ever knows where it would end but by the time it ends, you would know it. <br /><br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.devitareadsbooks.blogspot.com">www.devitareadsbooks.blogspot.com</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="16296509332668275295" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3243007201" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3243007201" />
    <a title="Sabina Knight" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/33677698-sabina-knight"><img alt="Sabina Knight" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1595853794p2/33677698.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3243007201?book_show_action=true">Mar 23, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sabina Knight" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sabina Knight" href="/user/show/33677698-sabina-knight">Sabina Knight</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/33677698-sabina-knight?shelf=chinese-literature">chinese-literature</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/33677698-sabina-knight?shelf=sex-gender-power">sex-gender-power</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3243007201" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5321628851649564054">_Shanghai Baby_ is powerful portrait of Shanghai, of a Chinese cosmopolitan caught between cultures and subcultures, and of a woman novelist still finding her unique voice. <br/><br/>The original has compelling philosophical and sociological aspects that are not evident in the English translation. Through no fault of the translator, who was constrained by market demands and his publisher, the English translation does not do justice to the original novel. Though fully competent, the translation changes the</span>
  <span id="freeText5321628851649564054" style="display:none">_Shanghai Baby_ is powerful portrait of Shanghai, of a Chinese cosmopolitan caught between cultures and subcultures, and of a woman novelist still finding her unique voice. <br /><br />The original has compelling philosophical and sociological aspects that are not evident in the English translation. Through no fault of the translator, who was constrained by market demands and his publisher, the English translation does not do justice to the original novel. Though fully competent, the translation changes the novel's tone, whitewashes or cuts key passages and cuts the entire postscript. The novel is uneven, but not consistently as low brow as the translation. <br /><br />The novel still inspires passionate responses and discussion among my Smith college students. This term we discussed the novel in #MeToo: Sex, Gender and Power across Cultures (WLT 276). <br /><br />In part in response to my students' enthusiasm, I wrote two articles treating the novel within the larger context of Shanghai and contemporary Chinese sensibilities:<br /><br />Sabina Knight, “Residual Romanticism in a Neoliberal Shanghai Novel.” _Frontiers of Literary Studies in China_, vol. 11, no. 1 (2017), pp. 106-132, doi: <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://10.3868/s010-006-017-0005-2">10.3868/s010-006-017-0005-2</a>.<br /><br />Sabina Knight, “Shanghai Cosmopolitan: Class, Gender and Cultural Citizenship in Weihui’s _Shanghai Baby_.” _China’s Literary and Cultural Scenes at the Turn of the 21st Century_, edited by Jie Lu, Routledge (2008), pp. 43-57. (Reprint with revisions of 2003 article.)<br /><br />Deirdre Sabina Knight, “Shanghai Cosmopolitan: Class, Gender and Cultural Citizenship in Weihui’s _Shanghai Babe_.” _Journal of Contemporary China_, vol. 12, no. 37 (2003), pp. 639-653, <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://doi.org/10.1080/1067056032000117687">https://doi.org/10.1080/1067056032000...</a>.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5321628851649564054" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_6185901" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/6185901" />
    <a title="Patty" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/347597-patty"><img alt="Patty" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1208265130p2/347597.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/6185901?book_show_action=true">Sep 14, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Patty" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Patty" href="/user/show/347597-patty">Patty</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer6185901" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8671721526313230126">I bought this book because of the controversy and upon reading it I understand why this book caused such a stir in China that drove them to burn the copies. Personally I found the topic quite ordinary, drug addiction and female sexuality is something that an army of Indonesian young writers love to discuss since the fall of New Order era. But given the fact that this is a book from a young woman in a country as repressed as China, I gotta give it some credit. The author was very brave. She spoke</span>
  <span id="freeText8671721526313230126" style="display:none">I bought this book because of the controversy and upon reading it I understand why this book caused such a stir in China that drove them to burn the copies. Personally I found the topic quite ordinary, drug addiction and female sexuality is something that an army of Indonesian young writers love to discuss since the fall of New Order era. But given the fact that this is a book from a young woman in a country as repressed as China, I gotta give it some credit. The author was very brave. She spoke openly and honestly and some of it was very raw I felt like peeping into someone's bedroom and caught some very private moments. I could not re-read it because doing so would make me feel that I've converted into perversion. However, I did wonder about one thing, is the courage to discuss female sexuality is really what signifies a country's freedom of speech? Indonesia has started it with Saman, and I guess Shanghai Baby is the Chinese equivalent. It gets a bit tiring and how I long for a brilliant female detective writer :p</span>
  <a data-text-id="8671721526313230126" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_16673620" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

<div id="review_3173690821" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3173690821" />
    <a title="Edward" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/78127591-edward"><img alt="Edward" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1521899734p2/78127591.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3173690821?book_show_action=true">Feb 09, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Edward" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Edward" href="/user/show/78127591-edward">Edward</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer3173690821&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3173690821" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15161454142528429263">I think 2 stars is kind, I would have given 1.5. The main character Coco is extremely unlikable - egotistical, vapid, obsessed with her looks, and immature. she longs for fame based on her "skill" as an author, and seems to be mostly motivated by that. not to become a good author for the sake of self development or because she has something she feels needs to be written, but just for fame. and it doesnt get much more shallow than that. Her man-baby boyfriend is also irritating due to his lazines</span>
  <span id="freeText15161454142528429263" style="display:none">I think 2 stars is kind, I would have given 1.5. The main character Coco is extremely unlikable - egotistical, vapid, obsessed with her looks, and immature. she longs for fame based on her "skill" as an author, and seems to be mostly motivated by that. not to become a good author for the sake of self development or because she has something she feels needs to be written, but just for fame. and it doesnt get much more shallow than that. Her man-baby boyfriend is also irritating due to his laziness and seeming lack of any vital life-force. The plot is unremarkable. It feels very clear that Coco is just a thinly veiled disguise for the author, which makes all the constant self congratulatory "I'm so talented", "I'm gorgeous" stuff all the more cringey. There is a lot of graphic description of sex, laying it on thick with ridiculous similes and comparisons (that I guess made sense to the author at the time), and this is what made the book so controversial as far as I can tell. but just because a book is controversial, does absolutely not guarantee it is an objectively good or entertaining read. Which is very clear here. The main things I liked about it are the brief insights into Shanghai culture I gained, and the fact it was short.<br /><br />Finally - the music choices that the characters listen to throughout the book seem unnaturally chosen in an attempt to make them seem cooler or more interesting. and, "The music was 'Dummy' by Portishead. People were dancing...". I'm sorry, people were dancing to that record?? Please.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15161454142528429263" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2987414340" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2987414340" />
    <a title="Tocotin" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4054892-tocotin"><img alt="Tocotin" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1280037257p2/4054892.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2987414340?book_show_action=true">Sep 23, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tocotin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tocotin" href="/user/show/4054892-tocotin">Tocotin</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/21000817-shanghai-baby">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4054892-tocotin?shelf=east-asia">east-asia</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4054892-tocotin?shelf=ebooks">ebooks</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4054892-tocotin?shelf=my-own-books">my-own-books</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4054892-tocotin?shelf=contemporary-literature">contemporary-literature</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4054892-tocotin?shelf=china">china</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4054892-tocotin?shelf=women-writers">women-writers</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2987414340" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13955229122606989662"><br/>I thought it was fun, fresh, and insightful. The best parts were the descriptions of various, famous and hidden, locations in Shanghai. I knew that this book had a bit of a scandalous reputation – not because it was especially racy, but because it was called empty, vapid, and pretentious, and its fame unmerited. Come on! So what if the narrator/author describes herself as super hot and talented? So what if she has both a Chinese and a foreign boyfriend, and loves to have sex with the latter, but</span>
  <span id="freeText13955229122606989662" style="display:none"><br />I thought it was fun, fresh, and insightful. The best parts were the descriptions of various, famous and hidden, locations in Shanghai. I knew that this book had a bit of a scandalous reputation – not because it was especially racy, but because it was called empty, vapid, and pretentious, and its fame unmerited. Come on! So what if the narrator/author describes herself as super hot and talented? So what if she has both a Chinese and a foreign boyfriend, and loves to have sex with the latter, but feels tender and protective towards the former? I thought she was sweet with both, and quite reasonable and level-headed about herself. It’s not that she was so completely engrossed in her love triangle that she didn’t notice others either – her friendship with Madonna, her warm relationship with her parents, and her interest in whoever approached her prove that she actually does have what it takes to be a writer.<br /><br />Madonna was my favorite character:<br /><br />“Boys today really know how to sweet-talk a girl. He just swore he’d die in my bed.’ She started laughing again. ‘True or not, it’s enough to keep me happy.’”<br /><br />And this was my favorite line:<br /><br />“From Daddy’s room came the sound of earnest debate, the men discussing some lofty topic of no practical importance.”</span>
  <a data-text-id="13955229122606989662" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('7KhoOGVB7H6ALiaIW89mi9Y0f9mgsf8lGR50glObo84gb105dIzy9zOGhAPsed4PW8L4M2Nb11u/9fMAkCj78Q==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

Assieme alle Confessioni di un italiano di Ippolito Nievo è considerato il più importante della letteratura italiana prima dell\'unità nazionale. Fu pubblicato in una prima versione nel 1827<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText6688294583506518527\" style=\"display:none\">I promessi sposi è un romanzo storico di Alessandro Manzoni. Assieme alle Confessioni di un italiano di Ippolito Nievo è considerato il più importante della letteratura italiana prima dell\'unità nazionale. Fu pubblicato in una prima versione nel 1827; rivisto in seguito dallo stesso autore, soprattutto nel linguaggio, fu ripubblicato nella versione definitiva fra il 1840 e il 1841.\nAmbientato dal 1628 al 1630 in Lombardia durante l\'occupazione spagnola, fu il primo esempio di romanzo storico della letteratura italiana. Secondo un\'interpretazione risorgimentista, il periodo storico era stato scelto da Manzoni con l\'intento di alludere al dominio austriaco sul nord Italia. Quella che Manzoni vuole descrivere è la società italiana di ogni tempo, con tutti i suoi difetti che tuttora mantiene.[1] Il romanzo si basa su una rigorosa ricerca storica e gli episodi del XVII secolo, come ad esempio le vicende della Monaca di Monza e la grande peste del 1629-1631, si fondano tutti su documenti d\'archivio e cronache dell\'epoca.\nIl romanzo di Manzoni viene considerato non solo una pietra miliare della letteratura italiana, ma anche un passaggio fondamentale nella nascita stessa della lingua italiana. Nei dialoghi, riporta anche diversi esempi di parlato spontaneo non ammissibili nella lingua standard. È una delle letture obbligate del sistema scolastico italiano.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"6688294583506518527\" href=\"#\" onclick=\"swapContent(\$(this));; return false;\">...more<\/a>\n\n <div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer1557970629069469310">Zhou Weihui (simplified Chinese: 周卫慧; traditional Chinese: 周衛慧) is a Chinese writer, living and working in Shanghai and New York. She is known in the West also as "Wei Hui".<br/><br/>Her novel <i>Shanghai Baby</i> (2000) was banned in the People's Republic of China as "decadent". Her latest novel <i>Marrying Buddha</i> (2005) was censored, modified and published in China under a modified title.<br/><br/>Wei Hui has been regarded b</span>
  <span id="freeText1557970629069469310" style="display:none">Zhou Weihui (simplified Chinese: 周卫慧; traditional Chinese: 周衛慧) is a Chinese writer, living and working in Shanghai and New York. She is known in the West also as "Wei Hui".<br /><br />Her novel <i>Shanghai Baby</i> (2000) was banned in the People's Republic of China as "decadent". Her latest novel <i>Marrying Buddha</i> (2005) was censored, modified and published in China under a modified title.<br /><br />Wei Hui has been regarded by international media as a spokeswoman of the new generation of Chinese young women. She has presented her work in a large number of Western publications, including The New York Times, The New Yorker, Time Magazine, CNN, USA Today, the BBC, The Times, The Sunday Times, the Economist, Stern, Welt am Sonntag, Asahi Shimbun, NHK, Yomiuri Shimbun, Le Monde, Le Figaro and more.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1557970629069469310" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
